## 6,242

6,242

B”H, 27 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I am in receipt of your letter from 22 Sivan, in which you write regarding your son, sheyichyeh, asking whether it is advisable to continue the current unclear situation in which he finds himself, etc., and also whether he should consider a separation, provided he first investigates whether another match will present itself, and only then decide whether or not to grant a get.

It is well known how much the Sages were concerned for the welfare of the daughters of Israel. From this, it is understood that the rationale of delaying the get until another match presents itself has no place whatsoever. On the contrary, when one avoids causing a woman to become an agunah, the blessings of Hashem will increase thereby.

Regarding the matter itself, since several years have already passed in the current state of affairs, one should now attempt once more (to satisfy all opinions) to bring the hearts of the couple closer together. If it becomes clear that there is no prospect for this, then in my opinion, there is no justification for keeping the woman in a state of agunah, which is of no benefit to either party. On the contrary, it only increases distress, etc. If only this had been done some time ago—but one does not cry over the past.

At an opportune time, I will mention all those you write about at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. May it be His will that you bring good tidings regarding each and every one of them.

With blessings for good news.

## 6,443

B"H, 27 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 15 Sivan, along with the enclosure and the *pidyon nefesh* that will be read at an *eis ratzon* at the holy *tziyun* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

You write regarding the health condition of Mrs. [Name], *tichyeh*. It is surprising that in all your letters you have not mentioned whether you have consulted the expert physicians in the field of gynecology, namely Prof. Asherman and Prof. Zondek, *shiyichyu*. Furthermore, I recall that there was a time when they would attempt to accelerate the healing of wounds by applying dressings containing certain vitamins that hasten the growth of tissue and skin, etc. It is possible that in recent years this is no longer used, but in any case, it is worth inquiring about this.

Regarding what you wrote—that in accordance with the doctor's instructions, the husband is compelled to use a condom, etc., since the wife is not to do anything in this matter—this is also surprising. You do not mention whether you have asked the opinion of a *rav* who is a *moreh hora'ah* in Israel regarding this. Moreover, if the *rav* is familiar with the overall matter, there are methods involving the woman that are not merely the use of chemical substances—which perhaps in such a case cannot be used—but also the matter of a *moch* (a barrier), an appropriate covering in the proper place, etc. Seemingly, in your case as well, it would be possible to use this, provided there is authorization from a *rav* regarding the matter as a whole.

Enclosed with your letter are the addresses of some of your relatives, and included is also an address in the United States. You do not specify whether you wish for me to contact this person to request assistance on your behalf. If that is the case, it is understood that you must elaborate further on the nature of your request, etc.

With blessings for good news regarding all that has been mentioned, with a strong sense of *bitachon* in the Creator and Guide of the world, and with an increase in *Torah* and *mitzvos*.

## 644

644

B"H, 27 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the Administration of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Lod
May Hashem preserve them and may they live

Peace and Blessing!

We acknowledge receipt of your letters dated 11, 17, 22, 23, and 24 Sivan, along with the enclosures and the building plans.

Regarding your main question, it has already been addressed in the previous letter; that is to say, as is customary in the world, the plan at the beginning of a fundraising campaign does not always obligate the actual execution later on, as the actual outcome depends on the results of the fundraising. Therefore, the initial plan should be made in a way that appears most favorable at the start of the fundraising, with the actual execution adjusted according to the results of the campaign.

Of the plans received now, the one where the buildings are in the shape of a *Beis* seems better to me than having all three buildings in a single row. However, it is understood that in this matter, too, one must take into account what is customary in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) and the impression it makes on the public.

Regarding what you wrote about the nature of the exterior walls, I have already written in my previous letter that it is surprising to see such distraction with details that should only be on the agenda near the completion of the construction.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## V'Shama

B"H, 27 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the Administration of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, Melbourne Branch
May Hashem preserve them and grant them life.

Peace and Blessing!

It is surprising that for a very long time, no reports have been received from you regarding your holy activities—not even regarding those activities you have already established. Furthermore, this is not enough, as we are commanded to always ascend in holiness. It is also surprising that reports have reached me from outside sources regarding differences of opinion concerning the program of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad’s work. This surprise is understandable, given that Tzeirei Agudas Chabad has been operating for quite some time in many countries, both in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) and abroad, and many letters have been written regarding this—what the goal of their work is. If only you would fulfill your role in accordance with this program; then you could place on the agenda the question of whether to add to these points.

According to what is explained at length in Chassidus regarding the concept of "the days were fashioned, and not one of them existed" (Tehillim 139:16)—the days allotted to a Jewish person, the number of days required for him to fulfill his mission in the world, corresponds to the matter of the Mishnah, "the number of days as required," no less, but also no more. The same applies to one’s energy. From this, it is understood that when one takes some of these days and energy and devotes them to matters that do not pertain to them, it is inevitable that there will be a deficiency in the fulfillment of their own task. How great the damage of this is, is also understood from the holy service in the Beis Hamikdash, which is the holiest of all work; the Torah—Toras Chaim—rules that a singer who performs the service of a gatekeeper, or a gatekeeper who performs the service of a singer, is liable for death, Heaven forbid. And this is well understood.

May it be Hashem’s will that you increase your energy as much as is required, and as is also necessary, in all the established areas of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad’s work. If you had done so during the past months, Tzeirei Agudas Chabad and its work—and indeed the face of Yiddishkeit in Australia in general—would have had an entirely different appearance. And when you do so now, at the very least, you are assured by our Rebbeim, the Nesi’ei Yisrael, that there will be abundant success.

And since we are approaching the days of the Chag HaGeulah, 12 and 13 Tammuz—a redemption that was entirely above the natural order—may you bring good tidings regarding all that has been said, that a change has occurred from one extreme to the other. And may it be above the natural order, provided only that it comes into actual practice.

Awaiting good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 680

B”H, 28 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Mr. Yitzchak Koren, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings,

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 17 Sivan, and I take this opportunity to emphasize my hope that our conversation will surely bring practical results regarding the points we discussed. Since the days that have passed since I became acquainted with you—and I am careful not to use the expression "by chance," for each of us believes that everything that occurs is by Hashgacha Pratis and not by chance—I have realized that you have even greater influence than I had initially imagined. Therefore, my hope has been further strengthened that you will utilize your influence to its fullest to instill the idea that those appointed by the people to manage matters that affect the entire nation, or broad segments of the nation, clearly should not be influenced by partisan considerations, nor even by party gains, regarding the practical management of affairs entrusted to them for the benefit of the entire people.

In other words, and in more pleasant terms: however significant the content and so forth may be in party matters, and as great as it is to be a representative of a party, the content is many times greater in matters concerning the entire nation and in being a representative of the entire nation. Furthermore, as we see tangibly, even during election periods, such leadership brings benefit to the party representatives even in terms of party gains.

Although you write that you will address several of the questions we clarified after your return to Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established, my hope is nonetheless that you will take an interest in addressing them even while you are here; for regarding several of them, one can hope for greater success by handling them while abroad.

With respect and blessing for good tidings.

## 6,347

B"H, 28 Sivan, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 20 Sivan, in which you ask my opinion regarding where you should study in the near future.

It is understood that it is most correct and best that you dedicate all your time to holy studies, especially at your age, where this is of the utmost importance. In the language of Chassidus, the brain in the head should be filled only with matters of our holy Torah—the Torah of life from the King of Kings, the Holy One, blessed be He. Regarding the matter of Talmud Torah, the halachic ruling is already well known: there is no room to postpone the matter of Torah study until tomorrow or the day after, since we are commanded regarding this study each and every day. Each day must suffice for itself, and one cannot make up what was lacking yesterday or the day before. In these latter generations, and especially in these years and in Eretz Yisrael—may it be rebuilt speedily—of which it is said, "The eyes of Hashem your G-d are upon it from the beginning of the year to the end of the year," there must also be a set order for the study of Pnimiyus HaTorah. This is explained in the holy Zohar, and even more specifically in the words of R. Chaim Vital (in his introduction to Shaar HaHakdamos), in the Iggeres HaKodesh of our Rebbe, the author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch, in Iggeres HaKodesh, Epistle 26, and in many other places. It is explained there as well how great this matter is and how necessary it is for success in the revealed aspects of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos.

Regarding your question on how to influence those you see drifting away from the path of Torah and mitzvos:

In such a case, the commandment "You shall surely rebuke" is well known, but it must be done in ways of pleasantness—rebuke that comes from Ahavas Yisrael. Then, one also succeeds to a greater degree and in a shorter amount of time. It is also understood that one should not be satisfied with speaking to them oneself, but rather one must find friends and acquaintances who will also influence them in the aforementioned manner.

Regarding what you wrote about distracting thoughts during davening: You should have your tefillin checked to ensure they are kosher according to the law. Furthermore, every weekday before morning davening, you should set aside a few coins for tzedakah. After davening each day, even on Shabbos and Yom Tov, you should recite the daily portion of Tehillim as it is divided for the days of the month. It is understood that all of this should be done without a neder.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 679

B"H, Erev Rosh Chodesh Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and learned, engaged in communal needs,
R' Ephraim Eliezer, may he live, HaKohen.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter of the 27th of Sivan.

Being at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, today, I mentioned you [in prayer].

May it be His will that you bring good news regarding both general and personal matters.

With blessings.

Regarding what you wrote concerning the filling of a tooth for a few months—concerning the issue of *chatzitzah* (interposition)—one should note the *Responsa* of the Tzemach Tzedek, *Yoreh Deah*, section 120, as well as the sources cited in *Darchei Teshuvah* to section 198, subsection 24, and *Tohoras Yisrael* there. Surely this has not escaped your notice.

In *Shaarei Tzion* (periodical, Jerusalem 1925), section 54, the author attempts to equate two opinions regarding the law that [a substance] one does not mind is not considered a *chatzitzah*—the opinion of the Rambam and Rashi—because one does not wish to remove it, due to [the concept of] *bitul* (nullification). According to the first approach, even in a temporary filling, one does not wish to remove it; on the contrary, one wishes to replace it with a more permanent one. (That is to say, that the flesh—which is what we are discussing regarding *chatzitzah*, not the object that is interposing—is covered more permanently.) And the handles of vessels that were inserted in an unusual manner are different, as there it must be a connection, and it is not [considered] a *chatzitzah*... (And I do not have the time to examine the aforementioned book to see if it discusses this reasoning). However, according to this: 1) Even a filling for a day or two should not be a *chatzitzah*. And this is not in accordance with many *Acharonim*. 2) In a temporary filling, on the contrary, one *does* mind it and wants to remove it (only not immediately), and therefore we must rely on the matter of connection and *bitul*.

By the way: Your note from the *Yerushalmi*, *Yoma* chapter 4, halacha 4, that permanence via a nail nullifies the *chatzitzah* of the casing, even though it was only for a day—Yom Kippur—does not seem correct to me, because there, it is a **permanent** fixture, as it is a fire-pan designated **only** for Yom Kippur.

***

**R' Ephraim Eliezer:** Yalles, Philadelphia. Additional letters to him—above, Vol. 16, 5791, and in the references noted in the footnotes there.

## 650

650

B"H, Erev Rosh Chodesh Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I am in receipt of your letter, in which you ask for an explanation regarding the connection between the mikveh and the activities of strengthening Yiddishkeit and disseminating Torah—specifically the dissemination of the wellsprings of Chassidus.

It is not understood what explanation is required. After all, you reside in the Holy City of Jerusalem—may it be rebuilt and established—which possesses an additional level of holiness. From this, a clear consequence follows: there must also be an increase in the holiness of the person, for an "arousal from below" is required to correspond to the "arousal from above." This increase in holiness is achieved through the matters of Torah and mitzvos and their inner dimensions—which is the concept of the "wellsprings."

This is also the concept of "Love your fellow as yourself"—meaning, not only that one has a "good eye" regarding his own affairs, but also regarding the affairs of others. In many areas, the aforementioned work of dissemination is essential, and certainly, the obligation rests upon everyone. From this, it is understood that the ability has been granted to everyone, and the matter depends only upon one's own will. And this is clear.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

## V'shna

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn

Mrs. Rivka, may she live and be well,

Blessings and peace!

I was pleased to receive your letter of the 27th of Sivan regarding the expansion of your group. May Hashem Yisbarach help that this should reveal within you the inner *kochos* that are granted to you from Above, to increase the group many times over, both in *kamus* (quantity) and *eichus* (quality). It is surely worth all the effort and *hishtadlus* that this requires, taking into account that every one of these women is not merely a *prat* (individual), but influences her entire home and family.

I was especially pleased to read in your letter that you have also begun an *agitatsia* among women who are about to be married. This is one of the most important matters, and as is known, the saying of our *Raboseinu Nesi’einu* is that the way one sets out is the way things proceed.

Regarding your question as to whether to come out publicly and also provide the name, that this is a branch of N'shei U'Bnos Chabad:

In my opinion, now during the summer, when the work slows down and the number of participants is smaller, is not the appropriate time to introduce something that might lead to discussions, at the very least from some of the *chaveiros*. It is more appropriate to wait until the work increases and picks up a faster *tempo*, and the news can be presented among other energetic initiatives, one after another, which will leave less time for those who wish to seek out *kushyos*.

At a *eis ratzon*, I will mention you, your husband, and your children, *shichyu*, at the *tziyun hakadosh* of *K'k Mo"ch Admur* *ztzukulleh"h nb"g"m zi"e*, that you should be able to report good tidings regarding your personal matters and the general matters, as one is dependent upon the other.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of *K'k Admur Shlita*,

Secretary

## 6,353

B"H, 1 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in communal affairs,
R' Shmuel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I was pleased to hear from my brother-in-law, the Rabbi, shlita, of the support and efforts made by you, may you live, when he visited our camp in Syracuse. Although for one such as yourself there is surely no need for an expression of thanks (especially according to the statement of our Sages that "the reward of a mitzvah is the mitzvah itself," and particularly according to the explanation in Chassidus that *mitzvah* comes from the word *tzavta*—connection and bonding—meaning that the true and greatest reward is the very performance of the mitzvah, through which a connection and bond is formed between the Commander, the Creator, and the created being, who are otherwise incomparable), nevertheless, it is appropriate to express the pleasure derived from your aforementioned attitude, and to express the hope that this attitude will not only continue but will grow, as it concerns the strengthening of a Torah institution, and we are commanded to "increase in holiness."

He also told me of your efforts in the discussions with Mr. Shapiro, may he live, and that there is a follow-up to this matter. Therefore, it is necessary to remain vigilant to ensure that the follow-up proceeds in an orderly fashion until its completion. The importance of this is already known from the statement of our Sages that a mitzvah is named after the one who completes it. Therefore, I am confident that you will do everything necessary in this regard, and even more.

As we stand in the month of Tammuz, the month of the redemption of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, may each and every one be redeemed—speedily and in our days—from all matters that distract them, and may there be an increase in blessing and success in all general and personal matters, for one is dependent upon the other.

With respect and blessings for good tidings.

***

**6,353**

**R' Shmuel:** Yalov, Syracuse. Additional letters to him: see Vol. 16, 6,144, and the references in the notes there.

**My brother-in-law:** My brother-in-law, the Rabbi, Shmaryahu Gurary.

*) Note the Jerusalem Talmud, Rosh Hashanah, end of chapter 1, that in order to connect the completion of a mitzvah to its beginning, it possesses the power to override the Shabbos that was present at its beginning.

## 6557

6557

B”H, 4 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and pious, etc., R’ Avraham Moshe, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 27 Sivan, in which you raise a difficulty regarding what I wrote in my previous letter concerning the Rambam’s view on Gan Eden, based on what the Rambam writes in his Commentary on the Mishnah to Sanhedrin—that Gan Eden is the choicest, richest, and most fertile land—and you cite what is written in various places regarding the matters of Gan Eden.

Although these matters are explained in several seforim, I will address the crux of the matter here, which is as follows:

There are several levels of Gan Eden. In this regard, Gan Eden at its lowest level is the Gan Eden spoken of in the simple meaning of the verses at the beginning of the Torah; it is in this world of ours, for a verse never departs from its simple meaning. It speaks there of fruits that are subject to physical consumption, and it is regarding this that the Rambam speaks in the aforementioned Commentary on the Mishnah. This Gan Eden was created together with the entire world, as per those verses, except that according to the words of the Rambam, it was hidden afterward and will be revealed in the time to come.

Above this is the Gan Eden that contains no matter or physicality, which is for souls without bodies. Generally, this category of Gan Eden is divided into two: the lower Gan Eden and the upper Gan Eden. Both are discussed at length in many places, including the Zohar, Part II, page 210 and onwards—see there, where it is proven that it is for the soul without a body. However, within each of these—the upper and lower Gan Eden—there are several levels, as is also explained in Likutei Torah, from which you quoted in your previous letter.

There is another matter, which is Olam HaBa. Regarding this, there is the dispute between the Rambam and the Ramban, brought in Likutei Torah by our Rebbe HaZaken, Parshas Tzav 15, 3. According to the Rambam, it is likewise a world of souls without bodies, and the text at the beginning of Perek Chelek—where it is evident that it refers to the soul with a body, see there—is a separate matter. According to the Ramban, Olam HaBa is for souls with bodies, and it is the very same concept as Techiyas HaMeisim. Based on what has been said, it is understood that there is no contradiction between the places you cite in your letter from one to the other.

Incidentally, I have written about this previously, and it is printed in Kovetz Lubavitch 9-10. It is surely available with someone in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt speedily.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 6258

B"H, 4 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 20 Sivan, with the *pidyon nefesh* enclosed, in which you write regarding the reasoning for performing surgery, but you do not specify the nature of the surgery—it is possible you mean the procedure known as a lobotomy—if so, it does not seem advisable to me, at least for the time being. For in the most frequent cases, the surgery has not been that successful thus far. From this, it is understood that one must attempt all possible methods beforehand. It is well known that regarding many of the pills and the various modern treatment methods currently in use, there are those whose effects are only noticeable after a year or two. It would be worthwhile for the attending physician to write in detail, in the manner of doctors writing to one another, regarding the treatment that has been administered until now, etc., and they should show this letter to the specialist in this field who is here.

May it be the will of Hashem that since we have entered the month of Tammuz—which is the month of the redemption of our Rebbe, the *Nasi* of Israel—and the body follows the head, may the entire body, in its full stature and down to the *ikvesa*, draw from the Head everything that is necessary. May each and every one be redeemed from all matters that cause confusion, and may you be able to serve Hashem with complete joy and a good heart.

## 6359

6359

B”H, 4 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

Following a long interval, your letter of 21 Sivan has been received.

Regarding what you wrote about talking during the reading of the Torah: it is astonishing that one needs to ask about such a matter from across the sea. It is even more astonishing that there are those who treat this lightly and bring forward strange proofs, after the words of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, are already known, and are also printed in his *Kovetz Michtavim*, at the beginning of which is *Yair Nasiv* by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, where he transcribes the words of his father, the Rebbe (Rashab) n”e.

His words are as follows: “It is clear that one who hears the reading of the Torah according to the law, besides the fact that he is fulfilling a mitzvah of Hashem, the very matter of the honor he shows the Torah causes the Torah to arouse mercy upon him and his household, both spiritually and physically.” End quote. And he opens that passage there: “They should be careful not to speak or converse during the reading of the Torah, etc.”

Regarding what you wrote about the *farbrengen* and what needs to be corrected in this regard: it is understood that it is not at all a matter where every small or large thing must be done by way of *kabalas ol*, as a decree or a statute. After all, even the Holy One, Blessed be He, in creating the world, imparted a portion of His Torah in a way that it would be understood through the intellect. How much more so, when one person is speaking to another within the Jewish people, there must also be understanding—especially when the discussion involves those who belong to Chabad. If even one who merely passes by a shop absorbs its scent, how much more so one who spends time and years [in such an environment], and sometimes even in ways where they were connected with *mesiras nefesh*, through which the matters become engraved within them, and in their inner dimension. It is almost certain that if the elders of *anash* would endeavor to explain the matters and the requirements, they would be accepted much more easily than one might anticipate. We have been promised that words which come from the heart enter the heart, especially since the merit of the many assists them, and particularly in Kfar Chabad, whose role—as has been stated many times—is to be a living example of a Chabad settlement. And this is clear. It is understood that all of the above also applies to the *shiurim* that are studied in public, etc.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

## 6366

B"H, 5 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, man of action, possessing fine character traits, etc.,
R' Yisroel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

It was with pleasure that I learned some time ago that you have begun to serve in the holy Yeshiva Tomchei Temimim, founded and directed by the Rebbe Rashab and his son, my father-in-law, the Rebbe. I was especially pleased to subsequently receive reports regarding the work being done with the students, may they live, and your letter from Erev Shabbos Kodesh was also received.

At an auspicious time, I will fulfill your request to mention you at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, for outstanding success in all matters of the Yeshiva, and specifically in the central and inner point: to raise students worthy of the name "Tamim" [see Arachin 31a, "Temimah—Rabbi says..." and the commentary of the Rambam; and Zohar Part III, 163b]. May this be together with the staff of the Yeshiva’s Roshei Yeshiva and Mashpi'im.

Regarding the dispute between Rabbi and the Sages in Arachin (ibid.): According to most commentators, for Rabbi, "Temimah" means that it completes the deficiency of the current year, but does not complete the deficiencies of other years; however, it has the virtue that it is always "temimah"—the solar year. In contrast, for the Sages, although its days are sometimes only the days of the lunar year, it possesses the virtue that at times it is not only complete in itself, but it completes and perfects the years that have passed, for this is the concept of the leap year (Ibbur).

In the avodah of Hashem, this is the distinction between the avodah of Tzaddikim (as explained in Tanya, that even a Beinoni, and certainly a Tzaddik, has never committed a sin and always walks his path securely) and the avodah of Baalei Teshuvah. When the latter are at their level, they also complete the deficiencies of the past, for their intentional sins are transformed into merits. In the words of our Sages, "If he was accustomed to reading one page, he shall now read two," similar to the analogy of a rope that is severed and then tied back together, where the knot makes it double and redoubled (see Iggeres HaTeshuvah by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, end of Chapter 9).

This is akin to the superiority of the teachings of Chassidus over the teachings of Mussar. As is known, the Baal Shem Tov gave an analogy regarding two ways to guard against a thief: either by shouting, "A thief is in the house!" to scare him away—in which case, after some time, he returns—or by strengthening oneself and capturing him until he no longer has the ability (or even the desire) to steal again. This is the same distinction between the teachings of Mussar and the teachings of Chassidus.

In these days preceding the days of the Chag HaGeulah, 12 and 13 Tammuz—the liberation of the director of the Yeshiva, my father-in-law, the Rebbe—and since the body follows the head, may it be the will of Hashem that each one of us be liberated immediately from all distracting matters, with a manifest victory over them, just as it was in those days thirty-one years ago. With an expanded mind, may you gather strength in all those matters for which the one celebrating this day sacrificed his life, first and foremost in raising students who fear Hashem, study His Torah in its entirety (both the revealed and the inner dimensions), and fulfill His mitzvos with beauty.

With respect and with a blessing for success in all the above, and for good tidings in this.

#### 6366

**R' Yisroel, may he live:** Grossman, Jerusalem. Another letter to him—above, Vol. 14, 5268.

**The analogy from the Baal Shem Tov:** See also above, Vol. 15, letter 5544.

6367

By the Grace of G-d
5 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Dr. Chanoch Reinhold
Greetings and Blessings,

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 30 Nissan, which was delayed in transit and arrived during the days associated with the festival of Shavuos. Please forgive the delay in my response, which is due to the pressure of my many responsibilities.

The subject of your letter concerns the educator’s relationship with the student’s family, particularly regarding students of Youth Aliyah who come from families in poor moral standing. The instructor must guide and mold the child toward a new path, and in doing so, finds himself undermining the child’s relationship with their parents and their connection to their family—especially regarding our Sephardic brethren, immigrants from Morocco, etc. You ask for my perspective on this.

In response to the above, one must first preface a fundamental point regarding youth in general: youth are generally not inclined toward compromise, and their worldview develops as a single, integrated whole.

As a result, when one challenges a part of that worldview and convinces them that it is based on a mistake, the entire structure is shaken. This is all the more true when one touches upon matters and assets that the student considers fundamental.

Anyone who observes the lives of our brethren from the Sephardic communities sees how their entire worldview—which has permeated their actions, speech, and thoughts in daily life from time immemorial until this very day—is an absolute faith in Torah, tradition, and mitzvos. This includes an absolute faith and complete obedience toward parents and teachers; these are the very things that dictate all their conduct, not only in matters between man and G-d, but also in matters between man and his fellow. It is only through this strong discipline—or, in the language of our Sages, this complete *kabalas ol*—that they have maintained their standing, both in the religious sense and in matters of good character, modesty, etc., in the countries where they lived until now. They did so under difficult conditions and in an environment full of trials, in a climate where the age of maturity arrives several years earlier than in Ashkenazic communities—the age of maturity with all the urges and trials associated with it.

This means that any touch, weakening, or deficiency in the aforementioned *kabalas ol* shakes the entire worldview of the immigrant from Morocco, Yemen, etc. It removes the restraint and the power of inhibition, leaving them to themselves in a constant struggle with the *yetzer*. The potential consequences of this are clear.

This is perhaps even more true in matters between man and his fellow than in matters between man and G-d, because ultimately, the fear of punishment, such as imprisonment and the like, is effective if it comes together with a worldview that affirms the holy, the good, and the moral, and rejects the opposite.

It is understood and simple that one cannot, in an instant, replace the discipline toward a father and the trust in him and one’s former teacher with discipline and trust in a new instructor who demands the opposite. For once a young man or woman has cast off discipline, they are not deterred from doing so a second or third time.

From all the above, my clear perspective is understood: until a worldview is prepared that is as grounded and established as necessary, and suited to the level of understanding of a specific youth, it is forbidden to touch the foundations of their current worldview. This is not the place to tear down in order to build later, for in the meantime, the youth in question is liable to descend lower and lower, without the possibility of raising them from the depths to which they have fallen.

Everything stated above does not touch upon the religious point of the matter at all, but rather concerns the educational and moral aspect, as it seems from your letter that this is what you intended. Even though your question is understandable—that it is impossible to leave the youth in the same state they were in, in a remote corner of Morocco, without any changes—it is also understood that one must not grasp the other extreme. That is, to toss them back and forth, to uproot all their roots of many generations, and only then begin to try to plant them in a culture that is utterly foreign and strange to them. The path here must be step by step, and one should not begin with the foundations. In other words, influence the youth through their parents and teachers, and to the extent that one must influence the youth directly, do so without touching the matters that are undoubtedly sacred in their eyes.

It is understood that this path is long, but the happiness of an entire generation depends on it—a happiness that requires psychological harmony, and through this, health in the simple sense and health in the sense of becoming a healthy part of society. It is worth it for the process of integration (in the way of life of our times, etc.) of our brethren from Morocco and Yemen, etc., to take several years longer, provided that they emerge from this integration healthy and whole—or at least, relatively whole.

This vital issue and painful subject requires much more length, but as mentioned, it is not possible to detail it as fully as necessary in a letter. I hope that as someone working in the field of education, these lines will suffice to clarify my perspective and also give expression to my deep sorrow over the alarming scandal that has prevailed among the youth of our brethren, the immigrants from Morocco and Yemen, etc., over the last few years. Every one of us within *Klal Yisrael* could justly have hoped and demanded that they serve as a replenishment for the hundreds of thousands of our brilliant youth who were destroyed in our generation—this generation of exile and double-darkness.

I hope there is no need to add the obvious remark that, G-d forbid, it is not my intention to cast aspersions or give a bad name to our Sephardic brethren, especially since I have learned much of their troubles and distress in the countries from which they came. Indeed, there are dozens of our own educational institutions there, headed by dedicated people who follow every detail of the students’ lives—not only during the hours they are in the institutions, but also in the hours before and after, and when they are at home and in the street. However, in order to rectify a situation, one must not close one’s eyes to the details of the situation. Closing one’s eyes is forbidden even if one intends to calm oneself or the surrounding environment, because by doing so, one weakens the energy for rectification and sometimes causes actions in the opposite direction.

I hope that you will utilize the talents with which you have been gifted by Divine Providence and the opportunities granted to you by your position in the Youth Aliyah department, so that their *aliyah* will be internal and true—not only to *Eretz Yisrael* in the sense of the physical place and territory, but also to the *Eretz Yisrael* of spirituality. An internal and complete *aliyah*, an *aliyah* of body and soul together. There is nothing that stands before the will. Especially since, after all, these children and youth are our brothers, our flesh, and in the words of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, author of the *Tanya* and *Shulchan Aruch*, their soul is a part of G-d above, truly. And regarding each and every one of them, the Creator and Guide of the world testifies: "My firstborn son, Israel," "You are children to Hashem your G-d."

With respect and blessing,

## 6,368

B"H, 6 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, engaged in communal needs,
R’ Refael Nachman, may he live, HaKohen

Peace and blessing!

It is surprising that you do not mention anything regarding the general affairs of Anash, which in truth **are** the conduit and vessel for the specific affairs of Anash. There is no difference whether one acknowledges this or not; in the style of the Rambam, opinions follow reality, not reality the opinions. Even though, as we have heard from our Rebbeim, Nesi’ei Yisrael, even if the general affairs did not impact or determine the specific affairs, one would still need to take an interest in them, to exert oneself and labor in their establishment and improvement, and this is understood. May it be the will of Hashem that you finally instill this awareness within each and every member of Anash, and then all the general affairs will take on a beautiful and radiant appearance, and immediately, an improvement will also be seen in the specific affairs.

We are currently in auspicious days, the days preceding the Chag HaGeulah of 12 and 13 Tammuz, the redemption of our Rebbe, Nasi Yisrael. And since the body follows the head, may it be the will of Hashem that throughout the entire stature, down to the very heels, there be drawn from the Head everything necessary, so that the stature may be complete and unified, and consequently, the redemption will also be complete and unified, and one will be able to serve Hashem with joy and a glad heart.

With blessings for good tidings and with the blessing of Chag HaGeulah.

## 6,369

6,369

B"H, 6 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shabbos Parshas Bamidbar and the one preceding it.

I believe I have already written to you, but in any event, it is the instruction of Chazal that one should not intervene between a husband and his wife, especially in the case mentioned, where in truth both are G-d-fearing and observers of mitzvos. If there are indeed certain details that require correction or, at the very least, improvement, it is better to influence this indirectly and specifically through pleasant and peaceful means. Furthermore, beyond this being the proper path, one can see tangibly that for the success of such influence, indirect and drawing-near approaches are more effective than anger or a manner of strictly demanding the letter of the law.

May it be Hashem’s will that for long, good years, you derive much true nachas—that is, Jewish, Chassidishe nachas—from all your descendants, may they live and be well.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 6170

B"H, 6 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you outline your background and mention that you teach music both in a school setting and to private students.

It is surely unnecessary to elaborate on the words of the Mishnah: "I was created to serve my Maker," which is accomplished through fulfilling the will of one's Maker by studying Torah and observing mitzvos. From this, it is understood that in whatever profession a person engages, it is certainly possible to disseminate true Yiddishkeit and its concepts among those with whom one comes into contact and connection. This is all the more so when the connection is that of a teacher and student, where every student naturally feels a sense of affinity and respect for their teacher. This also prepares the student to accept the teacher’s words even outside the scope of the specific subject matter, as is clearly evident. This is especially true given that, as you write, your students in this field are mostly youth, for whom any improvement and refinement of their situation is a refinement and improvement of their future, and first and foremost, in the building of their own Jewish homes.

From the above, it is understood that any effort and exertion in this regard is worthwhile.

Regarding what you wrote about entering to study at Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Lod, and detailing certain obstacles that are preventing you from doing so:

The practical question will be on the agenda in about a month or more, after the summer break, when the Yeshiva’s schedule is organized. It is possible that during this time, some of the details you mention in your letter will be resolved. Regarding your concern that you have debts, and therefore even after entering the Yeshiva you would need to continue your professional work in order to settle them—you will surely discuss this with the Yeshiva administration. There are many possibilities for arranging this in a way that will allow you to fulfill all the Yeshiva’s requirements, and it has already been said, "In the multitude of counselors there is salvation" (Mishlei 11:14).

May it be the will of the Creator of the world and its Guide, who oversees each and every person with Divine Providence, that you go from strength to strength on the path of Torah and mitzvos—the path that leads the Jewish man and woman to their true happiness, both materially and spiritually. I look forward to hearing good news from you regarding all of the above.

With blessings for good news,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

6170

## 6171

B.H., 7 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letters of the 24th and 25th of Sivan: It gave me pleasure to read in them, at the very least, an outline of the activities of the Tzeirei Agudas Chabad branch. I am certain that further reports will be received, and may it be Hashem’s will that their content be of good tidings—good for Heaven and good for people.

The letter to the students from the 15th of Sivan, 5711, should be publicized in an appropriate manner, as its content is relevant to every year, and there is no need for a new letter.

Regarding what you wrote about using a Chabad hall that is a subject of controversy, and the concern regarding the public’s reaction: The logic for using this hall is not understood, for what compels you to enter into a doubtful situation, especially in these months when the atmosphere is extremely tense?

At an *eis ratzon*, I will mention the students and all those you wrote about at the *tziyun hakadosh* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, navgam, zya*, each according to their respective needs.

I read with astonishment the conclusion of your letter, in which you state that you do not recite *V’shamru*, etc. This is the opposite of the custom of *anash* for generations. It is understood that the reasoning you provide for this is not at all sufficient to explain a practice that is contrary to our Rebbes, who instructed their students and those who follow in their footsteps. After asking your forgiveness, I must say that even according to the *nigleh* (the revealed Torah), this conduct is alarming, based on the instruction of our Sages regarding the conduct of a student toward his teacher (*Sukkah* 32b, "Rav Acha bar Rava would turn... since it came from his mouth," etc.). Regarding what you wrote about the lack of *semichus* (proximity) in any case in the *beis medrash* where you daven, there is room to say* that through contemplation in the interim on matters of *Geulah* or *tefillah*, the lack of *semichus* is nullified [note also the well-known dispute** regarding whether it must be immediate proximity or not]. This is not the case when one comes in the middle of a new matter, not a matter of *Geulah* or *tefillah*, and *kal v'chomer*.

At an *eis ratzon*, I will mention both of you at the *tziyun hakadosh* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, navgam, zya*, for that which you require. Surely there is no basis for overthinking, *chas v'shalom*, or even for the slightest bit of nervousness. Especially according to the explanations given at length and in detail—in a manner that is understandable even to human intellect—regarding the concept of *Hashgacha Pratis* from Hashem Yisbarach, who is the essence of good, the entire possibility of overthinking falls away.

It would be good if, in addition to the custom you surely maintain of *bnos Yisrael hakesheirot*—giving to *tzedakah* each time before lighting candles on *Erev Shabbos* and *Erev Yom Tov*—you would also do so every weekday morning until the coming *Rosh Hashanah*. Naturally, this should be *bli neder*.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above, and with blessings for the *Chag HaGeulah*.

**M. Schneerson**

6772

B"H, 7 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the Administration of Kfar Chabad,
May Hashem preserve them and grant them life,

Shalom u’Bracha!

These days, the minutes from the meeting of the Clarification Committee from Rosh Chodesh **Iyar**, and from the committee meeting of the 17th, etc., of Iyar and the 8th of Sivan were received.

The reasoning for rejecting organized marketing is not explained at all, especially after it has been discussed several times how necessary this is, etc. I have already written my opinion that one must utilize loans that can be utilized, for several reasons; and organized marketing, as you yourselves wrote, in addition to its general advantages, will also be helpful in obtaining loans.

A general note regarding certain items in the minutes (even though I believe I have already written this many times to various Chabad organizations): My response to whoever writes to me privately does not obligate the administration of an institution, an organization, or the administration of the Kfar in any way whatsoever. Only a response of mine that is received officially as a reply to the administration is binding. It is obvious that it makes no difference who the individual inquirer is; the point is whether this is a question from, or in the name of, those who represent the Kfar or the institution, etc., or if it is the question of a private individual describing the matter as it appears to him, etc. And this is understood.

Regarding your question as to whether, in the new housing project, you should build houses with four apartments or only two apartments—I have already written that one must strive as much as possible so that not only will the Kfar in general have a significant portion of farmers and agriculture, but that the new housing project specifically should also have an agricultural character. I also added that agriculture in this context does not necessarily mean grain field work, but also vegetables and the like. If, regarding the agricultural character, there is no difference between a house of four apartments or only two, then this plan should be discussed in accordance with the advantages one has over the other, as I have written regarding other matters of the Kfar. It is surprising that you do not mention those who, in a physical sense, are like them—and there are many such people in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt—and one must consider the results of the method of construction, etc.

I have received letters from several people who turned to the Kfar Committee with various requests, and the Committee told them to write directly to me. The astonishment at such conduct is understandable. However, since I am not responsible for the accuracy of the statement that the Committee instructed them to write, I have replied from here to every such inquiry that they must turn to the Kfar Committee. At most, one may send a copy of their request here, and nothing more.

In general, this is not the way—that every matter, whether large, medium, or even small, is not decided upon, and their immediate verdict is to send the question here. This is not the way to administer a Kfar or an institution.

With blessings for good tidings and success in the holy work of establishing and developing Kfar Chabad.

P.S. I received the letter from Rosh Chodesh with the attached minutes.

## 6373

6373

B"H, 7 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn

To the Avreich, Yitzchak, may he live.
Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 2 Tammuz, in which you write regarding the matter of Shabbos camps and the preparations for the vacation days.

It gave me pleasure to read in your letter about the idea to establish a program for intensive Torah study during the vacation days. The great benefit of this is understood and self-evident, and from this it is understood that it is worth the effort—even to the point of exertion—to bring this to fruition. May it be His will that you succeed in this. It is understood that this will add to the blessings of Hashem and to your success in your personal affairs, and through this, there will be progress and success in your own Torah study and the performance of mitzvos with hiddur.

Regarding your question about those who agree to study with you on the condition that you do not speak to them about practical matters:

This condition is strange. You can explain to them that even when speaking about practical matters, the choice remains **in their hands** to act as they decide. If so, they lose nothing by discussing these things with you. On the contrary, they gain, for it is possible that your words will contain new points and explanations they have not heard until now, and through this, the matter will be understood by them more clearly.

In any case, it is understood that even people such as these are obligated in Torah study, as this is a mitzvah in its own right. Therefore, you must teach them. As for what you mention regarding the words of our Sages concerning teaching Torah to a student who is not worthy, our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe—author of the Tanya (who rules on the hidden aspects of Torah) and the Shulchan Aruch (who rules on the revealed aspects of Torah)—has already written in his Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 4, Paragraph 3 of the Kuntres Acharon. He concludes there that the student (who is not worthy) himself is obligated to learn, etc., and the teacher as well, etc., if it is impossible otherwise. This is unless it is possible to return him to the right path by not admitting him to the Beis Medrash, in which case one pushes him away initially. The Alter Rebbe writes this even regarding one who is dismissive of mitzvos, lacks yiras shamayim, and does not fulfill what he learns even though he knows of the command. How much more so is this true for those you write about, where the cause is surely a lack of knowledge.

Regarding your question about the warning to teach Torah without compromises, and your concern that if you are strict with the children, perhaps they will not accept it at all—and that according to this, there is benefit in making compromises:

The advice for the aforementioned children is **not** to make compromises. That is to say, do not tell them that it is permitted, Heaven forbid, to transgress part of a mitzvah as long as they fulfill other mitzvos. For in addition to the prohibition of lying, [this] is also a matter of interpreting the Torah incorrectly, changing the religion, etc. Rather, explain to the children and clarify to them that for the time being, you are teaching them about this specific mitzvah, and they should know that there are many other mitzvos. However, it is impossible to teach them about all matters at the beginning, and therefore you teach them step by step. You should also explain to them the concept of teshuvah: that if, Heaven forbid, someone does not withstand the test and acts contrary to what is written, he remains obligated as before in **all** the mitzvos of the Torah, for "a Jew, even if he has sinned, remains a Jew," and furthermore, there is the rectification of teshuvah.

At a time of favor, I will mention you at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, may his merit protect us, for whatever you need.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 617

B"H, 8 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter in due time, in which you explain your situation as it appears to you personally. Regarding what you write at the beginning of the letter, you are absolutely correct that this is in the category of excuses and explanations. To this, you should hear that even if we were to assume they are accurate, they will not add anything to your study of Torah, etc., and there is nothing that stands in the way of the will.

As for the matters you detail that have caused you a sense of discouragement, the matter has already been addressed in the Tanya, in the explanation of the statement that "weeping is lodged in one side of the heart, and joy is lodged in the other side of the heart." It explains there how there is no contradiction between the two. The main point is that this knowledge is merely a means to reach the goal: the study of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos. The path to this—which is the path of truth you ask about—is to have the words of Rabbeinu HaZaken at the beginning of Chapter 41 of the Tanya engraved in your memory: "And behold, Hashem stands over him," etc. It would be very good to learn by heart from the beginning of this chapter until the word "HaMelech" (on page 2). This should bring joy to the heart of every person, as explained by the parable in the Tanya regarding a great king who lodges and dwells with a common, lowly person in his home (Tanya, Chapter 33). It is also understood that this lodging and dwelling is also a source of strength to conduct oneself in accordance with the King’s will. His will is explained in the Shulchan Aruch in minute detail; even regarding matters of personal choice, there is an entire chapter—Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim, Siman 231—based on the concept that "in all your ways, know Him."

May it be Hashem’s will that you do not dwell on how so-and-so treats you, etc., but rather know that you stand in a place of light: the Yeshiva of Tomchei Temimim, the perfect Torah of both the revealed and the inner dimensions. You are assured that if you truly desire it, you will succeed in the three pillars of Torah, avodah, and gemilas chasadim, upon which the world—both the world in the literal sense and the "small world" that is man—stands.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 6378

6378

B”H, 8 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Motzaei Shabbos, regarding your question: at your current place of employment, they are now facing layoffs, but your turn has not yet come. You ask whether you should resign voluntarily in order to receive severance pay and clear your debts.

It is somewhat surprising, as it is my understanding that in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), anyone who has held a position for years strives to keep it and is not eager for severance pay. After all, you will certainly not lose the severance pay even at a later time.

Incidentally, you mention that your wife (may she live and be well) did not want you to be involved in the field of chinuch. It is understood that this is surprising.

Surely you will explain her reasoning in your next letter.

Regarding what you wrote about participating in one-third of the travel expenses—we will participate, bli neder. Although, it is understandable why this is surprising, as it seems the situation in Tzeirei Agudas Chabad regarding funds is at the same point it was **several** years ago, with no movement for the better (and it is possible, on the contrary, that it is even worse). The surprise is even greater when considering that several organizations in Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) had no support when they were first founded, yet they became self-sustaining, including in financial matters. But, unfortunately, for Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, it has been years that we participate in their expenses, and there is no movement, etc. Perhaps there is some explanation: that, on the contrary, because they know there is an outlet for money that is partially available without effort, why strive to obtain it in another way? But it is understood that there is no justification for this, for two reasons: First, if they were to strive to obtain the means, they could expand their activities, etc. And second—and this is the main point—they surely realize that the funds taken by Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, which could perhaps have been obtained through their own efforts, are missing from other necessary matters.

It is understood that the above paragraph applies to all members of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, not only in Tel Aviv, but in the other branches as well [also regarding the N’shei U’Bnos Chabad organization, although for the latter there is a merit-based defense, according to our Sages’ statement that a kosher woman acts and conducts herself according to her husband’s will].

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

A. Quint
Secretary

## 6,380

Brooklyn.

Wolf, may he live, regarding his question on this]. You are surely preparing properly for the celebration of Chag HaGeulah, 12 and 13 Tammuz. May it be His will that you draw from them for all Chabad matters throughout the days of the year that follow.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 6,382

6,382

B”H, 9 Tammuz, 5718

Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, engaged in communal needs,
R’ Chaim Elazar, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

Regarding what you wrote concerning the *chilazon* and *techeiles* as it pertains to practical application today in *tzitzis*: It is well known that when this innovation emerged from Radzin, the greatest Torah authorities of the time raised an outcry and opposed it, even according to the *nigleh* (revealed) aspect of Torah. Furthermore, my father-in-law, the Rebbe (the Rebbe Rashab), of blessed memory, did not agree with this innovation in any way. I do not have his letter regarding this before me, but I recall that he brings that according to *Kabbalah*, the matter of *techeiles* will only be [relevant] in the time to come. And we have nothing to rely upon but the words of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

See also *Sukkah* 32b, bottom: “Rabbi Elazar was **metader** (careful/diligent)... since it came from the mouth of Rabbi Chanina.”

Regarding what you wrote about the *Mishnah* at the end of *Eduyos*, “A *mamzer* who is a *talmid chacham* takes precedence over an ignorant *Kohen Gadol*”: See *Zohar* Vol. III, 95b.

***

**6,382**

**R’ Chaim Elazar:** Haber, Petach Tikvah. Additional letters to him: see above 6,063, and the references in the notes there.

**Chilazon and Techeiles:** For an overview of the discussion on this topic, see *Kfar Chabad*, issue 400 (Erev Rosh Hashanah 5750), p. 29.

**His letter:** His *Igros Kodesh*, Vol. I, letter 158.

## 683

B"H, 11 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn

To the Administration of the Beis Rivkah Seminary, the Parents’ Committee, and the students of the Seminary in Kfar Chabad—the place where Hashem has commanded the blessing—may they live:

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters and the protocols of the administration and the parents’ meetings. It was a pleasure to read them, noting that you are all of one heart and one mind, in agreement that it is necessary to strengthen the Jewish and Chassidic education in the Seminary even more than it has been until now, and to increase the studies of holiness. Consequently, it is understood that since this depends on the teaching staff, you are interested in ensuring that the teachers are also suited for the aforementioned, so that their fear of Heaven precedes their wisdom.

Certainly, based on the above, each one—both from the administration and the teachers—will do everything in their power to achieve these goals. If our Sages said, "You have labored and you have found," regarding every place and every time, how much more so in our current time, when we see the success of efforts in matters of Torah and fear of Heaven, provided one engages in them with sincerity and the necessary energy. Especially in our Holy Land—may it be built and established by Mashiach Tzidkeinu—all of the above is more necessary than ever, and it is therefore understood that every effort and endeavor is worthwhile.

Because of the great value of this matter—and I refer to all matters in the field of education, for a change in the student leaves its mark throughout their entire life thereafter—it is understood and simple that any matter which casts doubt on the point of fear of Heaven and traditional Judaism is not worth entering into at all. Just as a *sfek-sfeika* (a double doubt) in the laws of *pikuach nefesh* (saving a life) is also forbidden, this is understood even by simple human common sense.

From this, my attitude toward books that are questionable from the perspective of fear of Heaven is understood, as well as toward teachers regarding whom there is doubt as to how they will influence the students in matters of fear of Heaven. To emphasize this further, I will add one more point: even though, as stated above, one must reject such books and teachers even in a situation where there is no alternative of other books and teachers—for first and foremost, the administration and certainly the parents must ensure that their students will be faithful to Hashem and His Torah, for this is their happiness in both the physical and spiritual realms—surely now one can find teachers and textbooks that are complete from the perspective of fear of Heaven (as you phrased it in your letter), and their wisdom will also be at an appropriate level. There are several Beis Yaakov schools in the Holy Land—may it be built and established—that, after passing through the initial period of uncertainty and doubt, have shown a path to solving the aforementioned problems based on the experience of several years. Surely the Beis Rivkah Seminary can also utilize the results of that experience. Since you wrote that you have also decided unanimously that fear must precede wisdom, as the Sages say in the Mishnah, it is surely unnecessary to add that this priority is not only in time but also in stature—meaning, that in a situation where one must choose between one teacher or textbook and another, where there is a difference between their fear of Heaven and their wisdom, the former must take precedence. It is also understood that regarding the points you mention, where in certain subjects the level of study was higher, in all that was said above, there is no intention to lower the level of studies if it does not touch upon matters of fear of Heaven. Surely there is no difference of opinion among you on this, and therefore it is somewhat unclear why you mention this in your letter as if there were some doubt about the matter.

Similarly, it is not clear from the letters regarding the point of the high school curriculum, which is mentioned several times. The Seminary is at the beginning of its founding, and despite this, it has succeeded—relatively speaking, at least. There is no logic in giving it the image of a high school, etc., and I believe I wrote this some time ago; it does not seem appropriate to me at all. The Seminary should maintain the character of a seminary for teachers and kindergarten teachers, similar to the seminary of [the] *Tafkid* [organization], which is most vital in our time. As for the concern you mention, that perhaps the relevant offices will not agree to recognize teachers without a matriculation certificate, there are good prospects that this, too, will change.

And the main thing is that even without the aforementioned recognition, there is a very broad field of activity for teachers and kindergarten teachers without it. As mentioned above, the main and most important thing is that the teachers and kindergarten teachers be one hundred percent suited for their most exalted task, and this suitability should not be diminished, even in the slightest degree, by changing the curriculum, changing the character of the Seminary, etc.

"And He rolls merit to the meritorious." I am writing this letter on the eve of the days of 12 and 13 Tammuz, the Redemption of our Rebbe, our Nasi, who fought—without looking at all the concealments and hiddenness—for the victory of traditional Chassidic Judaism and the pure Torah, and he was victorious openly before physical eyes, even though according to the way of nature there was no place for this.

The body follows the head; thus, each of his Chassidim, those connected to him, and those who belong to him, should walk in the path he taught us. Especially in matters where *mesiras nefesh* (self-sacrifice) is not required at all, but rather the surrender of the will of the *nefesh habehamis* (animal soul), to be complete with Hashem, the Creator of the world and its Guide, who watches over each and every one with *hashgacha pratis* (Divine Providence). Then the heart of each one will be complete to walk in the path of Torah and tradition, illuminated by Chassidic light and warmth, and to refrain and separate from any deviation and from any matter of doubt or *sfek-sfeika*, with strength and purity of purpose. Surely the blessings of Hashem will increase, and your success in achieving the aforementioned goal will come through expanded awareness and true happiness, both physically and spiritually.

In our matter, may the parents, teachers, and administration enjoy much true *nachas*—Jewish, Chassidic *nachas*—from all the students of the Seminary, and with justice and holy pride, they will be able to say, "See the great ones we have raised."

With blessings for good tidings in all the above, and with the blessing of the Festival of Redemption.

## 650

B"H, 13 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

After the longest of intervals, your letter from Erev Shabbos Shelach was received. As you requested, I will mention you at the holy resting place of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, regarding the matters you wrote about, and may it be Hashem’s will that you bring good news in this regard.

Regarding what you wrote about the *Kupas HaShanah* (annual charity fund), and your uncertainty as to how it works since you are contributing now in the middle of the year:

Your question is surprising, as it seems you have not seen the *sicha* regarding this charity. It explains that the matter is similar to what is discussed in *Maseches Shekalim* (beginning of chapter 2), which you mention, where they would contribute even for funds yet to be collected.

1. Simply put, one is included in these charity matters from Rosh Hashanah, even though one does not have the merit of *zerizus* (promptness), as the concept of *zerizus* is explained in many places and in *Iggeres HaKodesh* by our Alter Rebbe, Epistle 1.

Regarding your mention that you have endeavored to limit your eating time, etc.: it is surely unnecessary to remind you of the words of the Rambam (*Hilchos Deos*, beginning of chapter 4), that maintaining a healthy and sound body is one of the ways of Hashem. This is something we see tangibly: when the body’s health is not complete, it hinders one from engaging in Torah and *mitzvos* as one ought, and this is well understood.

You surely know the story of the *Chag HaGeulah* of the 12th and 13th of Tammuz—the redemption of our Rebbe, the leader of Israel. I trust you have participated or will participate in the *farbrengens* in connection with these auspicious days, and that you are keeping the *takanos* of the master of the celebration, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, by observing the three well-known daily *shiurim*: *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*.

With blessings for good news,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 680

B"H, 13 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in communal affairs,
R' Yechezkel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After a very long interval, I received your letter from the eve of 12 Tammuz, the *Chag HaGeulah* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

In it, you raise the issue of the *bas mitzvah* custom, which in recent years has penetrated even the circles of those who fear Hashem, to the point that it has almost become something impossible to abolish. The question is, rather, in what manner is it best to arrange it? You mention that it is customary to hold it on Friday evening—I assume you mean *Leil Shabbos*.

If I had the power, I would abolish this practice in this country from the outset. We are already distressed by the holding of *bar mitzvahs* on *Shabbos* due to the *krias haTorah*, and as is known, the obstacles that follow from this—leading to public conduct that is the opposite of *Shabbos* observance—all for the sake of marking a Jewish child entering the age of *ol mitzvos* and *ol Torah*. Based on the natural order of things, there is a great concern that by scheduling any celebration on *Leil Shabbos* or *Shabbos* day, even if one were to say that in certain cases it would not cause *chillul Shabbos* G-d forbid, there is no guarantee of this. This is true in the present time and in this place, and certainly in the future and in other places, where there is great doubt in the matter. *Kal v'chomer* and *dai l'meivin*. I have already spoken with several rabbis regarding the customs of their synagogues, that they must strive as much as is necessary to arrange *bar mitzvah* celebrations not on *Shabbos*, but on weekdays, for the reason mentioned above.

It is also understood that one must strive as much as possible that the scheduling should not be on Sunday. There is already enough *bizaion* and *ketzef* regarding many matters of the Jewish people that are pushed off to this day, even for the reason that the people are free from their commerce and business; but, incidentally, this lends some importance to their day of celebration, *r"l*. However, it seems that at present this is still in the category of a decree that the majority of the public here cannot withstand, and it is a great merit for them that the nature of their day of celebration has already been forgotten and no one pays it any mind. But regarding new matters, it is best not to enter into the matter to begin with and to arrange it on a day other than this, and the gates of excuses are not locked.

It is understood that I am not dwelling here on the general matter of a *bas mitzvah* celebration—and it seems this was not the subject of your question—but rather that in the current situation in your community, the question is only regarding the scheduling of the time.

Although the following note is surely superfluous, for the sake of emphasis I am putting it in writing due to the obstacles that have been caused in several places by the *bas mitzvah* ceremony. In some places, those who are *mehadrin* in the *bas mitzvah* ceremony raise their voices in song, and *b'avonoseinu harabim*, with the participation of women, primarily the *bas mitzvah* girl and her friends. In this, there is no room for compromise, for the law is explicitly stated in *Shulchan Aruch* as an absolute prohibition.

In connection with the news that reached me that you have joined the city council: may it be the will [of Hashem] that you utilize your influence—both that which you had until now and the influence added by this aforementioned appointment—to strengthen the resolve to introduce *Toras Emes* without compromises (for truth and compromise are absolute opposites) in the circles that are ever-expanding. This begins in your own community, where you serve in holiness officially, in the city where you have now joined the city council, and also outside of it. For all the Jewish people are one complete structure, and a deficiency in any one place affects all other places. And the *middah tovah* is greater, in that the addition [of influence] acts in every place. From this, it is understood that every effort and labor is worthwhile, for the matter concerns *Klal Yisrael* and its individuals. *Kal v'chomer*.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

With respect and blessing for good tidings in all the above.

## 6,191

B"H, 14 Tammuz, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from Motzaei Shabbos and the one following it.

At a time of favor, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy gravesite of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the content of your letter.

It is surely unnecessary to remind you that the more one increases in matters of Torah and mitzvos, the more one increases in the blessings of Hashem for everything that a person and his household require.

Regarding what you wrote about your wife’s (may she live and be well) visit to the doctor—it is surprising that you do not mention whether he instructed her to apply compresses. Very often, this alone resolves the entire issue, and perhaps it is worth asking the doctor about this now as well.

Regarding what you wrote—that you do not know what you want or what you are lacking:

Seemingly, this contradicts the beginning of your letter. Since you write that there are members of your household who are lacking in physical health and are in need of healing, etc.—and the words of Chazal are well known, that the 248 limbs of a person correspond to the 248 positive mitzvos, and the 365 sinews correspond to the 365 negative mitzvos—it is understood that a deficiency in the body must also be filled with the health of the neshamah. When one adds vitality to the neshamah, vitality is also added to the body in a literal sense, and success in medical treatment is increased. Furthermore, while matters of physical health are things for which we ask for Hashem’s healing and cure, for everything is in the hands of Heaven, regarding the health of the neshamah, our Torah—Torah of life—has informed us that, aside from yiras shamayim, the choice is given to man. From this, it is understood that there is nothing that can stand before a firm and strong will to ascend and add in Torah from strength to strength. May it be His will that you bring good tidings regarding all that has been mentioned.

You surely know of the three daily portions of Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya, which we have heard many times from my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, that they are appropriate for every soul and are a segulah for many matters.

It is also appropriate that you have the mezuzos in your home checked to ensure they are all kosher according to the law, and that your wife (may she live and be well) maintain the custom of the virtuous daughters of Israel to set aside coins for tzedakah before lighting candles every Erev Shabbos and Erev Yom Tov.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary
